class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12809%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFengping%2BXiao%252C%2BXuming%2BYang%252C%2BDonghong%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b22169%26title%3DMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFramework%2BDerived%2BCoS2%2BWrapped%2Bwith%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BLithium%252FSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12820%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2020" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0002.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Transition metal sulfides are attractive electrode materials for both lithium-ion (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Starting from micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O (IPC: 4-(imidazole-1-yl) phthalic acid) and polydopamine as the metal–organic framework (MOF) precursor and carbon source, respectively, we produced a CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite constituting CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles decorated with N-doped carbon layers and subjected to sulfurization. N-doped carbon layers provided a robust network for the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, enhancing the structural integrity and electronic conductivity of the resulting CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite, which exhibited electrochemical performance superior to most existing CoS<sub>2</sub> composites, and was one of the best among all MOF derived CoS<sub>2</sub> anodes for LIBs and SIBs. The use of nanosized CoS<sub>2</sub> particles reduced the diffusion length for the transfer of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in a high specific capacity at a higher current rate. N-doped carbon layers derived from the MOF precursor and polydopamine provided an electrically conductive network between the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, thus preventing their aggregation and inhibiting adverse side reactions between the electrolyte and the surface of the electrode, and the high pseudocapacitive contribution resulted in the enhanced rate performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12809%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFengping%2BXiao%252C%2BXuming%2BYang%252C%2BDonghong%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b22169%26title%3DMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFramework%2BDerived%2BCoS2%2BWrapped%2Bwith%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BEnhanced%2BLithium%252FSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12820%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292555&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Metal organic frameworks" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Metal%20organic%20frameworks" class="article__tags__link">Metal organic frameworks</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cobalt+Disulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cobalt%20disulfide">cobalt disulfide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal%E2%88%92organic+Frameworks&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%E2%88%92organic%20frameworks">metal−organic frameworks</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrasound-assisted+Reaction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrasound-assisted%20reaction">ultrasound-assisted reaction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium-ion%20battery">lithium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising energy storage devices due to their attractive features such as long cycle life and high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Nevertheless, present-day LIBs and SIBs are still facing some challenges in safety, cost, and service life. As such, optimized electrode materials have been sought for these batteries in the past decade. Various materials, such as transition metal sulfides/selenides/phosphides/oxides, have been widely studied. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> Among these, cobalt sulfide (CoS<sub>2</sub>) is a promising anode candidate due to its high theoretical capacity and ready availability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> However, it also possesses some drawbacks with respect to practical applications, such as pulverization during charge and discharge processes. Several attempts have been made to enhance its electrochemical performance by combining nanosized CoS<sub>2</sub> particles with a heteroatom-doped carbon matrix. Such an approach can bring the following advantages: (i) downsizing CoS<sub>2</sub> to nanoscale effectively reduces the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion distance and the mechanical stress during the conversion reaction; (ii) introducing the carbon substrate buffers the large volume change and prevents the agglomeration of CoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures; and (iii) heteroatom doping (mostly with N or S) improves conductivity and enlarges the interlayer distance of the carbon matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> Recently, Pan et al. developed N-doped polyhedral carbon-coated N-CoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanoparticles for SIBs, demonstrating a specific capacity of 766.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 100th cycle at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Nevertheless, the reliable fabrication of CoS<sub>2</sub> composite electrodes with high energy density and outstanding electrochemical performance is still within the focus of multiple research efforts.</div><div class="NLM_p">Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), a series of inorganic–organic coordination complexes, were extensively employed for various applications including catalysis, sensing, drug delivery, optoelectronics, and energy storage <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 15 to 25">(15−25)</a> due to their tunable pore size and structural diversity allowed by combinations of transition or rare-earth metal ions and organic ligands. However, owing to their poor electrical conductivity and low stability in organic electrolytes, pristine MOFs typically show rather poor electrochemical performance, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> so that their direct use as electrode materials in LIBs/SIBs is limited. Efforts have been focused on fabricating MOF derived nanoporous carbon and metal oxide/sulfide/selenide/phosphide/carbon composites through pyrolysis treatment. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 27 to 31">(27−31)</a> Several of the MOF derived materials showed improved electrical conductivity and structural stability, which, combined with variable chemical compositions and high surface areas, appeared promising for LIBs/SIBs. Recently, Bi et al. fabricated a NiS<sub>2</sub>@G composite (NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in graphene layers) through carbonization and sulfidation of a Ni-MOF, which conducted a specific capacity of 848 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> when used as an anode in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Zou et al. reported a new method to prepare bimetal-MOFs by directly utilizing CoSnO<sub>3</sub> as the precursor under solvent-free conditions. After being carbonized in Ar atmosphere, the obtained materials delivered a high reversible capacity of 493.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Previous studies on MOF derived electrode materials for batteries mostly relied on classical MOFs such as ZIF-8, ZIF-67, or MIL-125(Ti) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> with limited structural choices so that their properties could hardly be regulated by changing the ligands. Especially for cobalt sulfide, to the best of our knowledge, only ZIF-67 as the MOF precursor was employed so far. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref11 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref11 ref13" aria-label="reference 9, 11 and 13">(9,11,13)</a> Since the electrochemical performances of the resulting composites greatly depend on the structure of MOF precursors, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> it is worthwhile to develop and test novel MOF structures with promising properties for the resulting composite electrode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we first introduce the synthesis and structural characteristics of a previously unreported single-crystal bulk MOF material, namely Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O (IPC: 4-(imidazole-1-yl) phthalic acid). Because micron-sized or even nanosized particles are desirable for battery applications, we developed an ultrasound-assisted method to convert these bulk MOF crystals into micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O particles, with a high yield and high purity under mild conditions. After sulfurization, they were transformed into a composite material comprising CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in N-doped carbon (these samples are denoted as CoS<sub>2</sub>/C), which was then coated with another layer of N-doped carbon species derived from self-polymerized dopamine, resulting in the double-carbon-coated composite denoted as CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C. Combining the advantages of the large porosity and surface area of Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O, high capacity of CoS<sub>2</sub>, and efficient protection of this active material wrapped within two layers of N-doped carbon, the resulting CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C nanocomposite exhibited remarkable energy storage performance, which, to the best of our knowledge, is superior to most existing CoS<sub>2</sub> composites and one of the best among all MOF derived CoS<sub>2</sub> anodes for both LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis and Characterization of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p">Schematics of the synthetic strategy toward CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. A solvothermal method was employed to prepare single-crystal Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O bulk material by adding cobalt(II) nitrate hexahydrate, 4-(imidazole-1-yl) phthalic acid (IPC), dimethylformamide (DMF), and deionized water into an autoclave. A designated temperature program (details are given in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Section 4">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="4">4</a>) was used to grow large single crystals (with an average diameter of around 1 mm) suitable for X-ray single-crystal diffraction tests to determine their crystal structure. Lattice constants, crystal parameters, and simulated chemical structure of the Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O single crystal are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1 and Table S1</a>. It possesses a monoclinic crystal structure with a space group of <i><i>P</i></i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i>. The Co(II) ions are six-coordinated by IPC<sup>2–</sup> ligands. Each Co(II) ion is bridged with one N atom from the IPC<sup>2–</sup> ligand, two carboxylate O atoms from two different IPC<sup>2–</sup> ligands, and three O atoms from crystal water, generating a two-dimensional (2D) network. The bond lengths of Co–N and Co–O are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Scheme of the synthesis and the structure of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned above for battery applications, micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals with a large surface area are preferred. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> For the synthesis of micron-sized MOFs, the commonly used hydrothermal/solvothermal method has some drawbacks such as high energy consumption at a high temperature and long reaction time, and the product yield may be limited by the size of the autoclave, which impedes applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref37" aria-label="reference 29 and 37">(29,37)</a> Therefore, as a way of controlling the nucleation and crystallization process, an ultrasound-assisted room temperature treatment was developed here to prepare micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals in high yield and high purity with the product which shared the same crystal structure with bulk Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O MOFs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>). Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O microcrystals were further coated by polydopamine and converted to CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C nanoparticles in a sulfurization process.</div><div class="NLM_p">Morphologies of the micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals and the CoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials derived from the former were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O microcrystals had a shape of rodlike particles with an average length and width of ∼1 μm and 300 nm, respectively. After sulfurization of these microcrystals, CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite particles with an average diameter of around 120 nm were obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals under N<sub>2</sub> flow showed a large amount of mass loss from 400 to 600 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), which can be ascribed to the evaporation of organic ligands. This process leads to the shrinkage and agglomeration of the MOF, and thus the increase of the particle size of CoS<sub>2</sub>. As exemplified further in the TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d,e), a thin carbon layer, that was formed by pyrolysis of the organic groups from Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O microcrystals, was discerned at the edge of the particles. Elemental mapping of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite demonstrated well dispersed Co, S, and C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Interestingly, after wrapping with a polydopamine-derived N-doped carbon framework, unlike for the case of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite where particles experienced strong agglomeration (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite particles assembled into a distinct rodlike morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Moreover, a thicker carbon layer was observed at the edges of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C particles, demonstrating the wrapping of polydopamine-derived carbon over their surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g). Besides, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h, clear boundaries between the carbon layer (thickness of around 6 nm) and the crystallized CoS<sub>2</sub> were observed. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images showed lattice fringes of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f) and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>i) composites with <i>d</i>-spacings corresponding to the (200) and (210) lattice planes of CoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0005.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a) Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O microcrystals, (b) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and (c) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites, and HRTEM images of (d–f) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (g–i) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites at different magnifications.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>. They perfectly match the standard diffraction pattern of cubic CoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDF card no. 41-1471), confirming the high purity and crystallinity of the respective component in both CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites. No diffraction peaks from graphitic carbon were observed, which may be due to the weak crystallinity of carbon. To further characterize the type of carbon in these composites, Raman spectroscopy was conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). Both spectra of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites delivered two peaks at 1361 and 1586 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which correspond to the vibrations of sp<sup>3</sup>-type carbon (D-band) and sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon atoms in graphite (G-band), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The intensity ratio of the D-band to G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite (0.95) was higher than that of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite (0.91), suggesting that the wrapping of polydopamine-derived carbon layer created additional structural defects in the carbon component.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0007.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Raman spectra of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites. (b) X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey spectra of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites, and high-resolution XPS spectra of (c) Co 2p, (d) S 2p, (e) C 1s, and (f) N 1s elements in the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical states of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites were examined by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). As shown in the survey spectra of both composites, five peaks corresponding to Co, S, C, N, and O elements were present in both cases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). The O signal was presumably generated from the surface oxidation of the composites due to their exposure to air. High-resolution XPS spectra of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c–f. In the Co 2p core-level spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), two distinct peaks at 782.1 and 799.6 eV were assigned to the split 2p orbitals of Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, corresponding to CoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> The S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) could be fitted into three peaks at 163.4 eV (S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>), 164.2 eV (S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>), and 168.9 eV (the latter belonging to oxidized S, which was probably formed due to contact of the sample with air and oxygen). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e demonstrates the C 1s core-level spectrum, where three peaks could be deconvoluted at 284.8 eV (C–C bond), 285.9 eV (C–N bond), and 288.7 eV (C═N bond). The high-resolution N 1s spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) showed two distinct peaks at 400.7 and 398.7 eV, which could be assigned to pyrrolic N (C–N) and pyridinic N (C═N), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> The pyrrolic N (sp<sup>3</sup> hybridized) represented the N atoms, which were incorporated in the pentagonal rings, and the pyridinic N (sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized) atoms were located at the edges or in vacancies of the hexagonal carbon ring. The nitrogen doping contributes p electrons to the π-conjugated system of carbon, leading to the enhanced conductivity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0006.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cyclic voltammogram (CV) profiles of (a) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (b) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes used as anodes in Li half-cell at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of (c) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (d) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Cycle performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at various current densities. (g) Cycling performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first five cycles, as indicated on the frame).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The calculation of Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and total pore volume was based on the nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), giving values of 60.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 0.07 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite and 105.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 0.13 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite, respectively. To determine the contents of CoS<sub>2</sub> in the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites, TGA was performed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). After heating under airflow, the solid residues of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C constituted 51.2 and 39.7 wt %, respectively. Therefore, the weight ratio of CoS<sub>2</sub> in the two composites was quantified as 79.1 and 61.6 wt %, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrochemical Characteristics of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C Anodes in LIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate how the two composites would work as anodes for LIBs and SIBs, their electrochemical performances were examined. The CV profiles of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b. The main peak positions for both composites were similar; however, intensities of the reduction and oxidation peaks for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode decreased in the second cycle, indicating its poor cyclability. For the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, a broad peak at 1.31 V in the first cathodic scan could be assigned to the lithiation process of CoS<sub>2</sub> to Co and Li<sub>2</sub>S. The small peak at 0.78 V and the corresponding oxidation peak at 1.30 V may result from the formation and limited decomposition of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The small pair peaks at 0.38/0.53 V were related to the intercalation reaction between Li<sup>+</sup> and the carbon matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In the anodic scan, two intense peaks at 2.03 and 2.41 V could be assigned to the reversible oxidation reaction of Co to CoS<sub>2</sub>. CV profiles of the onward cycles nearly overlapped, suggesting the reversibility of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p">Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d. The initial charge and discharge capacities of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode were 873 and 1311 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 66.5%. Then, the capacity sharply decreased from the second cycle onward, and only 67 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Compared to the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, benefiting from the polydopamine-derived N-doped carbon wrapping to strengthen the composite structure, the cyclability of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode was greatly increased. The initial charge/discharge capacities were 1036/1324 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a CE of 78.3% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). Comparing the charge voltage profiles of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes, a slightly longer plateau between 2.1 and 2.4 V (due to the conversion reaction from Co<sup>0</sup> and Li<sub>2</sub>S to CoS<sub>2</sub> and Li<sup>+</sup>) was observed for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode. This suggests that the regeneration of CoS<sub>2</sub> was promoted by the presence of the additional N-doped carbon coating. The Coulombic efficiency increased to 95% at the third cycle and up to 99% from the fifth cycle onward, confirming the superior reversibility of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). A high reversible capacity of 1076 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was maintained for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode at the 100th cycle, which was much higher than that of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode. We note that the capacity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode initially slightly decreased to 891 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 20th cycle and gradually increased up to the 100th cycle. The corresponding cycle profiles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) reveal that the increase in discharge and charge capacities was observed below 0.7 V and above 2.5 V, respectively. According to previous studies, a large part of this capacity growth can be assigned to the “quasi-reversible” SEI formation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Rate performances of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode were also strongly enhanced as compared with the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>, for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, the average reversible specific capacity at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> were 1098, 1008, 915, 833, 740, 599, and 480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. A high reversible capacity of 1054 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was recovered at the returned 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, showing high reversibility for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode. In comparison, only 20 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was observed for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode at 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The long cycling stability of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode was evaluated under 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (with 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first to fifth cycles to activate the electrode). A high reversible capacity of 821 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was still observed at the 400th cycle with a capacity retention of about 96% (with respect to the sixth cycle) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g). Moreover, the Coulombic efficiency reached almost 100% from the 9th to the 400th cycle. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, such lithium storage performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode is superior to most of the reported cobalt sulfide-based electrodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">To explore the kinetics and the charge storage mechanism of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes, CV measurements at various scan rates (0.1–2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The positions of the main peaks of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode did not change with the increase of the scan rate, suggesting that the electrodes experienced only small polarization. According to previous studies, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> the degree of the pseudocapacitive contribution of an electrode can be determined from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>or<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where the values of <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> represent the current and the scan rate of the electrode, respectively, log <i>a</i> is the intercept of log <i>i</i> vs log <i>v</i> fitting line, and the value of <i>b</i> (in the range of 0.5–1.0) is the slope of this line. When the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, this would indicate that the system is mainly under a diffusion-dominated process, while this value close to 1.0 suggests a surface capacitive-controlled process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The fitted <i>b</i> values of peak 1 to peak 4 of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) were 1.05, 0.95, 0.84, and 0.86, respectively, indicating more contribution from surface reactions during cycling. Moreover, the quantitative contribution from the pseudocapacitive process could be calculated based on <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the current contribution from the capacitive charge and diffusion-controlled processes at a fixed potential, respectively, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> value can be obtained as the slope of the <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>)/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> vs <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> plotting line, and the <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> value is the intercept of this line. For the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c (the dark cyan area), the capacitive contribution was determined to be 84.7% at the scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and increased to 90.1% at the scan rate of 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The high pseudocapacitive contribution for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode enables fast Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reaction, resulting in improved rate capability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV profiles of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode used as an anode in LIBs at a scan rate from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Plots of peak current versus the corresponding sweep rate for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode. (c) Illustration of the pseudocapacitive fraction (dark cyan area) at the scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode. (d) Charge contribution percentage of the pseudocapacitive part to the whole capacity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.3.  Electrochemical Characteristics of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C Anodes in SIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites as anodes in SIBs were also evaluated, and the data are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b, CV curves of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode showed two cathodic peaks at 1.01 and 0.74 V, corresponding to the intercalation and conversion reactions between CoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The peak at 0.58 V could be assigned to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In the anodic sweep, three main peaks at 1.74,1.94, and 2.02 V corresponded to the reaction between Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S (1.74 and 1.94 V) and the regeneration of CoS<sub>2</sub> (2.02 V). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> CV curves recorded at the second and third cycles almost overlapped, demonstrating their excellent reversibility. For the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, a peak at 0.85 V in the first cathodic sweep was different from the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, which may be due to the variation of conversion steps, while other peaks at 0.58 V (cathodic sweep), 1.75, 1.93, and 2.02 V (anodic sweep) were the same as those observed for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0001.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. CV profiles of (a) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (b) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes used as anodes for the Na half-cell at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of (c) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (d) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Cycling performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at different current densities. (g) Cycling performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode at 1 A g <sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/medium/am9b22169_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0003.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. CV profiles of (a) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (b) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes used as anodes in SIBs from 0.1 to 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Plots of peak current versus the corresponding sweep rate of (c) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (d) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes. Percentage of the pseudocapacitive contribution (shown in dark cyan) to the overall recorded capacity of (e) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and (f) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/asset/images/large/am9b22169_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b22169&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Galvanostatic discharge–charge profiles of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes used as anodes in SIBs are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c,d. The discharge and charge curves of both electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> were consistent with the CV profiles. Tested in the carbonate-based electrolyte (1 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in propylene carbonate), the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode showed a rapid capacity decay, which is similar to the LIB results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>). The reversible capacity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode also faded gradually with only 506 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>). Benefited from the advantages of ether-based electrolyte where stable and thin SEI layer can be formed, and restrained dissolution of Na-polysulfides in this electrolyte, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a> both kinds of electrodes showed superior electrochemical properties. The CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode exhibited initial discharge and charge capacities of 871 and 778 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a CE of 89.3%, indicating a high active material utilization. However, its reversible capacity decreased gradually during the first 35 cycles and slightly increased in the following cycles, reaching 572 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 100th cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). Similar to the tests in LIBs, the increase of capacity could be ascribed to the partially reversible formation of the SEI layer, which mainly generated from lower (&lt;0.6 V) or higher (&gt;2.0 V) potential ranges (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Sodium storage performance of electrodes with different N-doped carbon coating contents, namely those denoted as CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-25 (coated with 25 mg of dopamine), CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C (which is the major sample discussed in this work, coated with 50 mg of dopamine), and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-100 (coated with 100 mg of dopamine), were studied. In the case of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-25 electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>), the reversible capacity decreased gradually, and only 501 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was observed after 50 cycles, which may be due to the insufficient coating by 25 mg of polydopamine. In contrast, both CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-100 electrodes demonstrated improved cycling stability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11b</a>). However, as compared with the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, the high coating of 100 mg of polydopamine in the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-100 sample increased its carbon content, leading to capacity decrease with initial discharge/charge capacities of 818/743 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which was lower than that for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode (861/798 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the corresponding CE of 92.1%). Such a high initial CE of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode could be attributed to the thin SEI layer generated in the ether-based electrolyte. When tested in the carbonate-based electrolyte, the initial CE for CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C was only 77% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>). The ether-based electrolyte has a lower reduction potential than the carbonate-based one, and thus, in the cycling process, a thinner SEI layer would be generated, due to the fewer amounts of decomposed organic products. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> We note that similar high values of initial CEs were reported for a number of cobalt sulfides in the ether-based electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref37 ref48 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref37 ref48 ref54" aria-label="reference 14, 37, 48 and 54">(14,37,48,54)</a> The Coulombic efficiency of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode increased fast to 99.8% in the second cycle and was maintained at around 100% in the following cycles, indicating its superior reversibility (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). Notably, high reversible capacity of 712 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was observed after 100 cycles, which outperformed most of the cobalt sulfide-based electrodes reported previously (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). After 100 cycles, the morphologies of particles of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes generally remained the same, albeit with some size expansion (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes showed that the charge transfer resistance was greatly reduced after the N–carbon coating (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). The capacity contributions of carbon components were studied for CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes in SIB tests with the initial charge capacity of 416 and 662 mAh (g<sub>carbon</sub>)<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S14 and S15</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f demonstrates the comparison of rate performances for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes at various current rates. A superior rate capability was obtained for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode, with average reversible capacities of 777, 711, 688, 671, 649, and 572 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The reversible capacity of 436 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was observed even at a rate of 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode showed obviously lower capacities at different rates with only 168 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the rate of 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The long cycle test for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode showed an impressive reversible capacity of 652 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 500th cycle with a high CE of nearly 100%.</div><div class="NLM_p last">We then studied the capacitive effect of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes in SIBs by CV tests from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a,b). The quantitative analysis of the capacitive effect was performed using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c,d, <i>b</i> values of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes were determined to be around 0.63 and 0.72, respectively, which indicated the joint effects of the diffusional and pseudocapacitive process. The ratios of the pseudocapacitive contributions were calculated from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>. The CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrode showed an increased pseudocapacitive contribution from 64.4 to 88.6% with the increase of the scan rate, which was higher than that of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e,f).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A novel MOF constituting the bulk single-crystal Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O with 4-(imidazole-1-yl) phthalic acid ligands has been synthesized and characterized. A room-temperature ultrasound-assisted method was used to prepare micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals, which shared the same crystal structure with the bulk MOF. Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O microcrystals were used as the precursor to form a CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite with CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in a thin carbon layer by direct sulfurization. This composite was further wrapped with a nitrogen-doped carbon layer that was derived from polydopamine, leading to the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite, which exhibited excellent lithium and sodium storage performance. In LIB tests, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C anode maintained a high reversible specific capacity of 1076 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 821 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a high rate performance of 480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. When employed as an anode for SIBs, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite also showed superior properties with a large reversible capacity of 712 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, accompanied by excellent cycling stability (652 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and rate performance (436 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>). The outstanding electrochemical performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes for both batteries could be attributed to the following reasons. First, the use of nanosized CoS<sub>2</sub> particles reduced the diffusion length for the transfer of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in a high specific capacity. Second, the N-doped carbon layers derived from the MOF precursor and polydopamine provided an electrically conductive network between the CoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, thus preventing their aggregation and inhibiting adverse side reactions between the electrolyte and the surface of the electrode. Moreover, after wrapping the N-doped carbon layer around CoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanoparticles, the high pseudocapacitive contribution to the capacity resulted in the enhanced rate performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes. Our study introduced a synthetic method to prepare transition metal sulfide from the novel micron-sized MOF precursor and promoted the research on transition metal sulfides as electrode materials for LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">4.1.  Synthesis of Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O Single Crystals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (0.0292 g, Sigma-Aldrich, 98%) and 0.1 mmol 4-(imidazole-1-yl) phthalic acid (H<sub>2</sub>IPC, 0.0232 g) were sequentially added into a mixture of 5 mL of dimethylformamide (DMF) and 5 mL of deionized water while stirring and transferred into a 25 mL autoclave and subjected to thermal treatment by increasing the temperature to 150 °C in 12 h and maintaining this temperature for 48 h, followed by 48 h cooling to room temperature. Purple single crystals were obtained, centrifuged, and washed with ethanol twice and dried at 40 °C overnight. The reaction yield of the Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O single-crystal product was 46 wt %. The CCDC number is 1945818.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">4.2.  Synthesis of Micron-Sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O Crystals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals were synthesized by an ultrasound-assisted method. H<sub>2</sub>IPC (0.232 g, 1.0 mmol) was dispersed in 120 mL of ethanol by ultrasonication, followed by the addition of 20 mL of 0.1 mol/L NaOH. Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (0.292 g, 1.0 mmol) dissolved in 10 mL of ethanol was added with ultrasonication for 30 min. The precipitate was centrifuged, washed with ethanol twice, and dried at 40 °C overnight. The pink powder product was obtained with a reaction yield of 87 wt %.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">4.3.  Synthesis of Micron-Sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O Crystals Wrapped with Polydopamine</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals (100 mg) were dissolved in 40 mL of deionized water by ultrasonic treatment for 15 min (solution A). Dopamine hydrochloride (50 mg) was dissolved in 80 mL of the Tris-buffer solution (pH 8.5) under stirring for 15 min (solution B). Solution A was drapply added into solution B and stirred for 4 h to fully polymerize the dopamine. The black powder of the polydopamine-wrapped Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O was collected by centrifugation and dried overnight. For comparison, micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals were also wrapped with 25 mg and 100 mg of dopamine hydrochloride.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">4.4.  Synthesis of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To synthesize the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite, 0.2 g of micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals were loaded into a tubelike sample holder and placed in the center of the quartz tube. An 8-fold mass of sulfur powder was loaded in another tubelike holder and placed in the upstream of the quartz tube. The quartz tube was heated to 600 °C under a temperature increase of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and kept for 2 h in a slow flowing Ar gas. After reaction and cooling, the sample was further washed with CCl<sub>4</sub> to remove the unreacted sulfur from the surface of the sample. The CoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite was obtained with a reaction yield of 60 wt %. The CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite was synthesized by one-step carbonization and sulfurization of the polydopamine-wrapped Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O under the same experimental conditions and procedures as CoS<sub>2</sub>/C. CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-25 and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C-100 composites were derived from the corresponding 25 and 100 mg of dopamine-wrapped micron-sized Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O crystals via the same procedures of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C.</div></div><div id="sec4_100" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16">4.5.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Single-crystal X-ray data for Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O were measured at 296 K using a Bruker D8 Venture X-ray equipment with Mo/Kα radiation (λ = 0.71073 Å). The structure of Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O was solved by the SHELXS-2014 program. Morphologies of the materials were characterized by a field-emission scanning electron microscope (FEI Quanta FEG 450) and a (high-resolution) transmission electron microscope (JEM-2100F operating at 200 kV). To explore the crystal structures and chemical compositions of the materials, powder XRD (D2 PHASER, Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5406 Å), Raman spectroscopy (Renishaw InVia, 532 nm laser), TGA (Mettler Toledo thermogravimetric analyzer), and XPS (VG ESCALAB 220i-XL) were employed. The BET specific surface area and pore size distribution were measured based on the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms (Autosorb-iQ, Quantachrome).</div></div><div id="sec4_200" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17">4.6.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composite materials were tested as active materials in the anodes for LIBs and SIBs. The battery fabrication procedures were as follows. Active materials were mixed with acetylene black and CMC (sodium carboxymethyl cellulose) aqueous solution (5 wt %) with a ratio of 7:1.5:1.5 by grinding to form a homogenous slurry, which was evenly coated on a copper foil and dried at 80 °C for 3 h. Then, the dried foil was punched into 16 mm round disks and vacuum-dried at 110 °C for 5 h with areal mass loading of active materials varying from 1.0 to 1.4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. An Ar-filled glove box was used to assemble the CR2032 coin cells for both battery tests with Li/Na metal foils as reference and counter electrodes and glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) as separators. The electrolyte for LIB was 1 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (6:4 v/v). For SIB tests, the electrolyte was 1 M NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME). Galvanostatic tests were measured from the Neware battery testing system. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) were performed on the VMP3 (Bio-Logic) electrochemical workstation.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22169?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b22169</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Structure characterization and TGA of the Co(IPC)·H<sub>2</sub>O single crystals; TEM elemental mapping, XRD, BET, TGA data of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C composites; additional electrochemical tests for the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C/C electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b22169/suppl_file/am9b22169_si_001.pdf">am9b22169_si_001.pdf (1.47 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b22169" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Leveraging valuable synergies by combining alloying and conversion for lithium-ion anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bresser, Dominic; Passerini, Stefano; Scrosati, Bruno</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3348-3367</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The essential need for new lithium-ion battery materials providing higher energy and power densities has triggered an exceptional increase in scientific and industrial research efforts in recent years.  Regarding the anode side, the two major research directions to achieve improved energy densities have, so far, focused on materials which can host lithium either by alloying or by a conversion mechanism.  Very recently, however, a new class of potential next generation anodes is gaining continuously increasing attention: conversion/alloying materials.  Herein, we provide for the first time a comprehensive review on this new materials' class.  Initially, we discuss the two possible approaches to realize a combined conversion and alloying mechanism in a single compd., starting either from pure conversion or pure alloying materials.  Based on this overview we subsequently highlight the fundamental insights and their potential advantages, which shall provide scientists with some general considerations and principles for the development of new, further enhanced conversion/alloying materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrLHnFiRwnB4LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liodfsA_tJR0A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFeqsrfF&amp;md5=6eb72e859ae67c8a48493172088e1f8d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700606"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering Hierarchical Hollow Nickel Sulfide Spheres for High-Performance Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Dan; Sun, Wenping; Zhang, Yu; Dou, Yuhai; Jiang, Yinzhu; Dou, Shi Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for energy storage due to the abundance of sodium, esp. for grid distribution systems.  The practical implementation of SIBs, however, is severely hindered by their low energy d. and poor cycling stability due to the poor electrochem. performance of the existing electrodes.  Here, to achieve high-capacity and durable sodium storage with good rate capability, hierarchical hollow NiS spheres with porous shells composed of nanoparticles are designed and synthesized by tuning the reaction parameters.  The formation mechanism of this unique structure is systematically investigated, which is clearly revealed to be Ostwald ripening mechanism on the basis of the time-dependent morphol. evolution.  The hierarchical hollow structure provides sufficient electrode/electrolyte contact, shortened Na+ diffusion pathways, and high strain-tolerance capability.  The hollow NiS spheres deliver high reversible capacity (683.8 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1), excellent rate capability (337.4 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1), and good cycling stability (499.9 mAh g-1 with 73% retention after 50 cycles at 0.1 A g-1).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqILcValYXCmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgxCd2O4oOa5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVymtrbN&amp;md5=df1523f34e777d5f61481d30ef557f22</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.01.064"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct conversion of metal-organic frameworks into selenium/selenide/carbon composites with high sodium storage capacity</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Xuming; Wang, Shuo; Yu, Denis Y. W.; Rogach, Andrey L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">58</span>
